Noise Characterization of Deep-UV Gas-Filled Hollow Core Fiber Laser 
 
Callum R. Smith1,*, Asbjørn Moltke1, Abubakar I. Adamu1, Mattia Michieletto2, Patrick Bowen2, Peter M. 
Moselund2, Christos Markos1, Ole Bang1,2  
1. DTU Fotonik, Technical University of Denmark, Kgs. Lyngby, DK-2800, Denmark  
2. NKT Photonics A/S, Blokken 84, Birkerød, DK-3460, Denmark  
*caros@fotonik.dtu.dk 
 
Abstract 
The realization of an ultraviolet (UV) laser source exhibiting wavelength tunability and excellent noise properties 
would significantly benefit the scientific community. Here we characterize the noise of a deep-UV source based on 
an argon-filled hollow-core (HC) anti-resonant (AR) fiber. Pump pulses coupled into the AR fiber experience soliton 
dynamics and undergo extreme spectral broadening creating a supercontinuum, leading to efficient energy transfer to 
the phase-matched resonant dispersive wave (RDW) in the UV spectral region. Suitable pump pulse are produced 
using a commercially available laser centered on 1030 nm followed by a nonlinear compression stage. This system is 
capable of producing pulses with 30 fs pulse duration, 24.2 µJ pulse energy at 100 kHz repetition rate with a pulse-
to-pulse relative intensity noise (RIN) of < 1%. The center wavelength of the RDW could be tuned between 236 – 377 
nm by adjusting the argon pressure. Under optimal pump conditions the pulse-to-pulse relative intensity noise (RIN) 
was measured to be 1.9% for a RDW centered on 282 nm. The RIN is resolved spectrally for the pump pulses and the 
generated RDW and supercontinuum. 
 
1. Introduction 
The scientific community would benefit greatly from a compact, wavelength tunable laser source capable of delivering 
ultrashort UV pulses. Furthermore, in order to maximize performance within an intended application it is important 
that the pulse-to-pulse RIN is as low as possible. Applications that would directly benefit include wafer inspection for 
semiconductor manufacturing [1], time resolved pump-probe measurements [2], angle-resolved photoemission 
spectroscopy [3] and seeding of free-electron lasers [4]. The compact nature of the desired laser would set it apart 
from large and complex established UV sources, which include synchrotrons, free-electron lasers and frequency 
conversion systems. 
A promising approach to achieve a tunable UV laser is to utilize gas-filled HC fibers. HC photonic-crystal fibers 
(PCFs) which provide broadband guidance, for example those with a Kagomé lattice [5] or negative curvature AR 
structure [6], are particularly applicable to ultrashort pulse experiments. These fibers provide a low anomalous 
dispersion environment across the transmission band. Combining a HC fiber with a gas-cell allows control over the 
gas species and pressure within the core. Furthermore, the normal dispersion of the filling gas interacts with the 
anomalous dispersion of the HC fiber to allow dispersion control within the fiber core. Through control of gas species 
and pressure the zero-dispersion wavelength (ZDW) can be tuned across the transmission band. Coupling a pulse of 
sufficient energy, and with frequency within the anomalous region of the fiber, will initiate soliton self-compression 
resulting in extreme spectral broadening. A RDW can be excited if the spectral broadening reaches the phase matching 
frequency, defined as the frequency at which the propagation constants of the soliton and the RDW are equal. 
Consequently, an efficient transfer of energy from the soliton to the RDW ensues. The phase-matching frequency 
occurs on the other side of the ZDW, in the normal dispersion region of the fiber. Furthermore, the position of the 
RDW can be tuned by varying the gas properties within the HC fiber. 
This concept has been employed utilizing a range of gas species to generate UV radiation with broadband guiding 
HC PCFs [7-12] and with simple capillary fibers [13]. The majority of these experiments relied on a titanium-sapphire 
laser to provide the pump pulses, resulting an inherently large footprint and low repetition rate of 1 kHz to achieve the 
required pulse energy. However, one experiment utilizes the nonlinearly compressed output of a commercially 
available ytterbium-doped fiber laser, which significantly increased the repetition rate to 1.92 MHz [12], an important 
consideration for maximizing signal-to-noise ratio in certain applications. Furthermore, the use of an ytterbium-doped 
gain medium paves the way to a more compact design, owing to the increasingly outstanding performance of these 
lasers at high repetition rates [14, 15]. Interestingly, despite the impressive results achieved using this technique, the 
pulse-to-pulse stability of the generated UV radiation has not achieved considerable attention. Obtaining UV radiation 
with low RIN is extremely important to maximise performance within applications.  
==PAGE:177==
Here, we present an investigation into the RIN of deep-UV radiation generated using RDW excitation in an argon-
filled HC AR fiber, demonstrating a value of < 2% can be achieved under appropriate pumping conditions. To generate 
suitable pump pulses we employ a commercially available fiber laser (Origami 10XP, NKT Photonics A/S) operating 
at 100kHz, combined with a nonlinear compression stage based on an air-filled HC AR fiber. This high repetition rate, 
compact format, combined with a detailed noise investigation, takes the concept of a tabletop, tunable deep-UV source 
closer to a commercial reality. Section 2 discusses issues relating to noise measurements, and describes how we define 
and measure RIN. In section 3 we describe the full experimental details of the nonlinear compression stage. We 
characterize the pump pulses in terms of pulse energy, pulse duration and RIN. Additionally we measure the spectrally 
resolved RIN of the pump pulses. Section 4 presents a full characterization of the tunable, deep-UV radiation, covering 
RIN, tunability, power and beam profile. Furthermore, we will discuss how these results compare to simulations. 
Finally, in section 5 we present our conclusions.  
 
2. Relative Intensity Noise 
Noise is a very important property and is required to establish whether a laser is suitable for a particular application. 
Unfortunately, there are numerous definitions of noise, requiring different experimental techniques. This makes it 
difficult to compare quoted noise values, especially when there is a lack of information regarding the definition and 
technique used. In this section we explain how we define noise using RIN, and we describe how we conduct noise 
measurements. Without a clear understanding of what is meant by noise, and how it was measured, little confidence 
can be given to a quoted value. 
We conduct these RIN measurements using a fast silicon photodiode (DET10A2, Thorlabs, Inc.) connected to an 
oscilloscope (HDO9404, Teledyne LeCroy, Inc), as shown in the general experimental set-up in fig. 1. The laser 
initially undergoes some operations, which can include nonlinear interactions and spectral filtering, either through the 
use of dielectric-coated spectral filters, or a grating-based monochromator. Following these operations, the laser beam 
is attenuated to a  power level suitable for the photodiode. For high powers, this consists of taking one or more 
reflections from an uncoated wedge followed by transmission through a series of neutral density filters. The laser 
beam is subsequently focused onto the detector, so that its power falls entirely within the active area. We have found 
that RIN varies considerably across the spatial profile of the beam, therefore it is very important to ensure the whole 
beam area is sampled. The photodiode generates a photocurrent in proportion to the optical power on the detector, 
which is consequently converted into a voltage and displayed on the oscilloscope. Thus, the pulse train creates a series 
of voltage peaks at the laser repetition frequency. The rise time of the photodiode is 1 ns and thus several orders of 
magnitude greater than the pulse durations being considered in this paper. Therefore, the length of each voltage peak 
is a characteristic of the photodiode. It is important that each pulse is clearly distinguishable from the next. 
Furthermore, it is absolutely essential that the photodiode is operating in a linear response regime. 
 
Fig. 1 Schematic of experimental arrangement for measuring RIN.  
 
To calculate RIN we require the voltage measurement of a sample of pulses. The oscilloscope runs a MATLAB script 
to record the voltage response from an individual pulse, as shown for an example pulse in fig. 2 (a). Once this process 
is complete the script finds a new pulse and again records the voltage response. This process continues until a pre-
determined number of pulses have been recorded. A voltage value is then assigned to each pulse, given by Vpulse, which 
is calculated by taking the voltage difference between the peak of the pulse and the pulse floor, as indicated in fig. 2 
(a). Thus, an array of voltages is obtained, corresponding to the relative power of each pulse. A typical histogram 
==PAGE:178==
showing the distribution of Vpulse for 2000 pulses is shown in fig. 2 (b). Here we can see the data conforms to a normal 
distribution. The example data given in fig. 2 is taken from the direct output of the Origami 10XP operating at 35 µJ.  
 
 
Fig. 2 Example pulse trace (a), example histogram of Vpulse (b).  
 
The RIN is calculated according to: 
 
!"# = 100 × )
* 
Eqn. 1 
where σ is the standard deviation and µ is the mean of the array of Vpulse. Thus, the RIN defines the percentage change 
in power (positive or negative) compared to the mean that will contain one standard deviation, which for a normal 
distribution corresponds to 34.1% of the data. The calculated RIN from the example given in fig. 2 is 0.5%. We 
maintain this definition and experimental technique for all the RIN measurements presented in this paper. 
 
3. Nonlinear Pulse Compression 
The commercial laser used in this investigation was operated at 100kHz, producing pulses with a duration of 400 fs, 
a pulse energy of 35 µJ and a RIN of 0.5% at a center wavelength of 1030 nm. This pulse duration is too long to 
initiate the required soliton dynamics to achieve RDW generation in our experimental arrangement. Therefore, 
nonlinear pulse compression was employed to generate pulses of suitable duration. This common technique utilizes a 
Kerr medium, whereby the spectral bandwidth of the pulse is increased through self-phase modulation (SPM), creating 
a positive linear chirp across at least the central portion of the pulse. This chirp can then be compensated by a linear 
dispersive element providing anomalous dispersion, leading to temporal compression.  
The first stage of this two sage process requires a suitable nonlinear material. Solid-core silica fiber is not possible 
since the peak powers involved (~ 80 MW) are significantly higher than the critical power for self-focusing (~ 4 MW), 
thus laser-induced damage is inevitable. Systems which achieve spectral broadening of such high peak power pulses 
in a non-guiding bulk medium have been reported, however these systems have complex multi-pass [16] or multi-
component [17] architectures, and suffer from inherent spatial distortions due to the non-guiding nature [18]. 
Therefore, gas-filled HC fiber was deemed the appropriate nonlinear medium, since it provides a simple and compact 
compression stage with high damage limit and negates spatial distortions. To further simplify the system, the fiber 
was operated without the use of a gas-cell, and was thus simply filled with laboratory air at atmospheric pressure, 
similar to the approach present in [19]. A 375 mm length of HC AR fiber (provided by NKT Photonics A/S) with 27 
µm core diameter and 780 nm capillary wall thickness (CWT) was employed to provide the required spectral 
broadening. Note that the ZDW of this fiber was approximately 940 nm, and thus the fiber was pumped in the 
anomalous dispersion region, which is not ideal from optical pulse compression. A pair of identical plano-convex 
lenses with 50 mm focal length were used to couple light into the fiber and collimate light out of the fiber. Temporal 
compression was achieved with a pair of dispersive mirrors (HD59, Ultrafast Innovations GmbH). Each mirror 
==PAGE:179==
reflection provides a group delay dispersion of ~ -500 fs2, with a total of four reflections used in our system. A 
schematic of the nonlinear compression stage is shown in fig. 3 (left), with a scanning electron microscope (SEM) 
image of the fiber end-facet shown (inset).  
 
 
Fig. 3 Experimental set-up, L1, L2, L3 = UV-fused silica plano-convex lens, OAP = off-axis parabolic mirror, DM = dispersive 
mirror. 
 
The performance of the compression stage was characterized (fig. 4 (a)) by recording the incident and transmitted 
average power with a thermal power meter, the compressed pulse duration was measured with an intensity 
autocorrelator, and the RIN was measured as described in section 2. An overall transmission efficiency of 69% was 
achieved, providing a maximum output pulse energy of 24.2 µJ. The compressed pulse duration at this pulse energy 
is 30fs, corresponding to a compression factor of 13.3. The fiber length was empirically chosen such that maximum 
pulse compression occurred at maximum available pulse energy, thus allowing maximum peak power. The RIN of 
the compressed pulses tends to increase with pulse energy, however it is maintained at < 1% across the full range. Fig. 
4 (b) shows an intensity autocorrelation trace of the compressed pulse at 20.9 µJ, which corresponds to a pulse duration 
of 34.3 fs. In fig. 4 (c) the pulse spectrum is shown, alongside the spectrally-resolved RIN, obtained using a 
monochromator to isolate the spectrum at ~ 2 nm increments with an approximate ~ 3 nm full-width half-maximum 
(FWHM). The power spectral density has the features of classical SPM, with additional power distorting the profile 
between ~ 1020 – 1035 nm. This power is attributed to cladding light, which can occur from non-perfect coupling, or 
scattering within the fiber length, and undergoes different spectral broadening to the core light, thus creating the 
observed spectral feature.  
 
 
 
 
Fig. 4 Pump pulse duration, RIN and energy (a), intensity autocorrelation trace (b) and spectrum with spectrally-resolved RIN 
(c). 
==PAGE:180==
SPM increases the spectral bandwidth of the pulses in proportion to the coupled power [20]. Thus variations in the 
power coupled to the fiber will result in more or less spectral broadening, resulting in a constant variation of the 
FWHM of the broadened spectrum. Therefore, frequencies on the edge of the spectrum have high noise values, since 
the power here varies considerably with changes in the spectral bandwidth. Furthermore, this effect becomes more 
pronounced towards the edges of the spectrum, thus explaining the increasing RIN at the spectral wings. Despite 
measuring a total RIN of 0.94% (fig. 4 (a)), the RIN varies considerably as a function of frequency, within a range of 
0.6 – 13% across the full spectrum. This detailed data on the pump pulse RIN could allow for improved modelling, 
and ultimately a better understanding of the noise properties of subsequently generated spectra. 
 
4. UV Characterization 
Fig. 3 (right) shows a schematic of the UV generation stage. For RDW generation we utilized a HC AR fiber (provided 
by NKT Photonics A/S) sealed in a monolithic gas-cell. The fiber (end-facet shown in fig. 3 (right, inset)) has a 50 
µm core diameter, 860 nm CWT and 140 mm length. The gas-cell can be filled with Argon from 1 – 20 bar. Argon 
was selected since it is a noble gas and thus Raman-free, simplifying the nonlinear processes leading to RDW 
generation [11]. An input and output window, constructed from anti-reflection coated UV fused silica and uncoated 
calcium fluoride respectively, allow the laser beam to enter and exit the gas-cell. The pules from the nonlinear 
compression stage are coupled into the fiber using a plano-convex lens with a focal length of 100 mm. The output 
beam is collimated using an aluminium-coated off-axis parabolic (OAP) mirror and sent on to diagnostic equipment. 
At maximum pump pulse energy the total transmission efficiency at the fiber output was 70%, corresponding to an 
average power of 1.69 W. This measurement factors in the losses associated with the gas-cell output window and OAP 
mirror. 
To analyze the output spectrum the beam was directed into an integrating sphere, which was fiber-coupled to a 
CCD-based spectrometer. Clear RDW generation was observed for a range of Argon pressures and pump conditions. 
Increasing the pressure increases the nonlinearity within the fiber, and therefore the peak power required to achieve a 
clean RDW decreased. Fig. 5 (a) shows how the RDW peak can be tuned with pressure, with pump conditions chosen 
at each pressure to achieve the cleanest RDW. The lowest pressure that allowed clean excitation was 6.5 bar, which 
required pump pulses with 24.2 µJ energy and 30 fs duration to excite a RDW peak at 236 nm. As pressure increases 
the RDW peak redshifts, which a maximum peak wavelength of 377 nm for pump pulses with 18µJ energy and 45 fs 
duration.  
 
 
Fig. 5 RDW relationship with argon pressure (a) and full spectrum at 10 bar of argon with spectrally-resolved RIN (b). 
 
To measure the RIN of the RDW we used a deep-UV spectral bandpass filter centered on 280 nm with a FWHM of 
10 nm. To tune the RDW to match the spectral filter, the argon pressure was adjusted to 10 bar and the pump pulse 
conditions were set at 20.9 µJ energy and 34.3 fs duration, which are the parameters presented in fig. 4 (b, c). This 
resulted in a RDW center wavelength of 277 nm. The full spectrum generated under these conditions is shown in fig. 
5 (b). The spectrum shows strong peaks at 578 nm and 880 nm, which are the result of resonances defined by the 
CWT. The RDW can clearly been observed, albeit with a reduced power spectral density compared to other features 
of the spectrum. The spectral bandpass filter was employed to reject all wavelengths outside the transmission band, 
==PAGE:181==
allowing direct access to the central frequency components of the RDW. This deep-UV radiation was focused onto 
the photodiode using an uncoated calcium fluoride lens with 50mm focal length. The RIN of this radiation was 
measured to be 2.7%. Additionally, this measurement was repeated at different wavelengths using spectral filters 
across the spectral bandwidth, as shown in fig. 5 (b). There are four distinct regions where low RIN, i.e. < 3 %, was 
observed: the RDW, the 578nm resonance, the 880nm resonance and the 1000 – 1050 nm band corresponding to the 
wavelengths present in the pump pulse. ***Further discussion on this is required – talk to Asbjørn regarding 
simulations and explanations***.  
The RIN of the RDW centered around 280 nm was further investigated by varying the pump pulse conditions. The 
pump energy threshold for RDW generation was 19.2 µJ, and clean RDW generation was observed up to 22.1 µJ, 
above which the spectra became distorted with additional spectral peaks in the UV region. The center wavelength of 
the RDW blueshifts as the pump energy is increased over this range, as observed in fig. 6 (a), which also shows the 
FWHM transmission band of the 280 nm spectral filter (shaded gray). The evolution of power in the UV region, i.e. 
< 400 nm, was measured by spatially separating frequency components using a calcium fluoride prism, allowing UV 
radiation to be isolated and directed to a thermal power meter. As observed in fig. 6 (b), the power initially increases 
rapidly around the threshold pump energy, reaching a maximum level of 35.4 mW between 20.4 – 20.9 µJ, before 
decreasing slightly and leveling off at higher energies. This power measurement level accounts for losses of the gas-
cell output window, the OAP mirror and the prism. Thus, the power conversion to the UV from the compressed pump 
pulses is 2.1%, with an estimated 42% of that UV power contained in the RDW peak. Therefore, the RDW has an 
average power of ~ 15 mW, and a pulse energy of ~ 150 nJ, corresponding to a total conversion efficiency of 0.5% 
through the entire system of pulse compression and RDW generation. This conversion efficiency could be improved 
by reducing the CWT such that the number of resonances and their impact is reduced. Further improvements could 
result from improving the performance of the nonlinear compression system, by operating it with a nonlinear medium 
which exhibits normal dispersion at the pump wavelength. Fig. 6 (a) also shows the RIN of the radiation transmitted 
by the spectral bandpass filter, which at threshold is measured at 11.5 %. Initially, increasing the pump energy results 
in a decreasing RIN, and a minimum value of 1.9 % is reached at 20.4 µJ, after which a further increase in pump 
energy leads to an increasing RIN, up to a value of 15.8 % at 22.1 µJ. The high RIN measured at the lowest pump 
energy can be explained by the fact that the RDW process is close to threshold conditions, and thus small changes in 
pump conditions can have a pronounced effect on the RDW generation process. Thus, the initial decrease follows that 
as the RDW generation process increases above the threshold energy the stability improves, hence slight fluctuations 
in the pump conditions have less impact on the RIN. The subsequent increase can be attributed to the fact that the 
RDW center wavelength moves out of the FWHM transmission band of the 280 nm spectral filter, so the radiation 
being sampled by the photodiode is no longer from the central section of the RDW but actually from the wings of the 
peak and background UV radiation. As observed in fig. 5 (b), the RIN increases rapidly as the wavelength is increased 
from the RDW central wavelength. Therefore, it can be seen that a careful choice of pump conditions is necessary to 
achieve clean RDW generation with low-noise properties.  
 
 
Fig. 6 RIN and RDW center wavelength (RDW CW) (a) and UV power (b) as a function of pump pulse energy. 
==PAGE:182==
The beam was profiled after the 280 nm spectral filter using a CMOS image sensor, which despite the low responsivity 
in the deep-UV was sufficient to acquire the image shown in fig. 6 (b, inset), taken for a pump pulse energy of 20.4 
µJ. The beam displays a Gaussian profile, indicating the beam quality is high, which is desirable in most applications.  
 
5. Conclusion 
In this paper we present a compact laser source generating low-noise, tunable, deep-UV radiation. In section 2 we 
stress the importance of defining noise measurements, and outline the experimental technique we use to conduct these 
measurements. Section 3 describes a simple nonlinear compression system utilizing an air-filled HC AR fiber. This 
system achieves 69% transmission efficiency with a compression factor of 13.3, generating pulses up to 24.2 µJ with 
30 fs pulse duration. The RIN of the compressed pulses are spectrally resolved, and it is found that the RIN of the 
frequency components across the spectrum can vary significantly. In section 4 the output from the compression system 
is used to seed RDW generation in a second HC AR fiber sealed within an argon-filled gas-cell. The center wavelength 
of the RDW is shown to be tunable from 236 nm to 377 nm. The system is tuned so the RDW center wavelength 
overlaps with a 280 nm spectral filter. The RIN across the whole spectrum is characterized, with regions of low RIN 
(< 3%) observed at the RDW, at the resonances due to the CWT, and at the pump wavelength. The RIN is further 
characterized as a function of pump pulse energy, and it is observed that the RIN decreases as the RDW generation 
process is increased above threshold. It is also noted that the center wavelength of the RDW blueshifts with increasing 
energy, and therefore in order to achieve low-noise radiation at a specific wavelength, careful attention must be paid 
to pump pulse parameters. For an optimal pump pulse energy of 20.4 µJ, a RIN of 1.9% is achieved. The generated 
deep-UV beam profile is shown to closely match that of the fundamental Gaussian mode. The ability to generate deep-
UV radiation at 100kHz repetition rates with low-noise and high spatial quality in a compact format will be of 
extremely useful in applications such as semiconductor metrology and spectroscopy. Furthermore, with pulse energies 
in the region of 150 nJ, and with further improvements predicted through optimized fiber design, this source could 
find applications within photo-acoustic microscopy.
